    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d71e15a713534dcf80fb3a4ea51a6875">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f6b117ab-f3ff-4b41-adfd-28fbfd112f50'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

data-db-target-for="article" data-db-switch="icon-close_thin" aria-haspopup="true" aria-controls="article_Pop" role="button" id="article_Ctrl" class="coolBar__ctrl hidden-lg"><i class="icon-abstract"></i><span>About</span></a><div data-db-target-of="article" aria-labelledby="article_Ctrl" id="article_Pop" class="coolBar__drop fixed rlist dropBlock__holder"><div article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn figures-tab cloned-with-ids empty"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-multi-figure"></i><h2>Figures</h2></a></li><li role="presentation"    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d14617619" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Because direct carbonation of asphalt usually yields ordered graphite structure with unfavorable storage of sodium. Here, the asphalt preoxidation at a specific temperature in the air introduces oxygen-containing groups to connect the unsaturated aliphatic hydrocarbon and aromatic side chains, forming a disordered carbon skeleton to inhibit melting and rearrangement during carbonization. The abundant oxygen-containing groups hinder the growth of the carbon layers during pyrolysis, which promotes the formation of disordered phases and abundant micropores in asphalt-based hard carbons (HCs). The simultaneous increase in initial coulombic efficiency, capacity, and transport behavior of sodium ions in HCs is achieved by adjusting the carbon layer and micropore evolution. The optimized HCs display excellent initial coulombic efficiency of 86.14% with remarkable reversible capacity of 313.83&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and high-rate capability with 140&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C. Pairing with O3-NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode, the full cell delivers a higher reversible capacity of 255.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial coulombic efficiency of 83.7% and long cycle life. Based on the microstructure and electrochemical behaviors of asphalt-based HCs, the “adsorption-insertion-pores-filling” sodium storage mechanism is proposed, providing guidelines for designing high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403084-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403084-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as a highly promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in large-scale applications for the grid and renewable energy storage because of the low cost, wide distribution, and natural abundance of sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0001_R_d14617610e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0002_R_d14617610e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, sodium ions, possessing a larger radius in comparison to lithium ions, exhibit the weaker kinetic characteristics of sodiation/desodiation, resulting in some anode materials used in LIBs being unsuitable for application in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0003_R_d14617610e387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">3</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0004_R_d14617610e387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbons (HCs), with abundant micropores and large carbon layer spacing, provide a superior storage capacity of ≈300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and low operation voltage, making them stand out from the crowd of anode materials for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0005_R_d14617610e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0006_R_d14617610e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Energy">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, HCs are mainly obtained from various plants and polymers by high-temperature carbonization, but the low-carbon rate and high-cost characteristics limit their commercial application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0007_R_d14617610e400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Fuels">7</a>-<a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0011_R_d14617610e400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Appl. Phys. Rev.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As one of the by-products of the chemical industry, asphalt is recognized as an excellent precursor for HCs preparation due to its advantages of easy availability, low price, and high carbon yield.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0012_R_d14617610e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">12</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0013_R_d14617610e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Fuel">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, asphalt, predominantly composed of 2D alkyl cyclic hydrocarbons and aromatic compounds, is carbonized directly at high temperatures to easily form carbon sheets with smaller interlayer spacing (3.4 ≤ d<sub>002</sub> ≤ 3.7), showing unsatisfactory properties with lower initial coulomb efficiency (ICE) of 15% and Na-storage capacity of 130&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0014_R_d14617610e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Mater. Chem.">14</a>-<a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0017_R_d14617610e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, inhibiting graphitization during carbonation becomes a crucial factor in improving the Na-storage capacity of asphalt-based HCs in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Asphalt preoxygenation (APO) represents an effective strategy for inhibiting graphitization during pyrolysis to improve the sodium storage capacity of asphalt-based HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0012_R_d14617610e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">12</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0018_R_d14617610e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Wang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0019_R_d14617610e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> used HNO<sub>3</sub> solution as an oxygen source to introduce crosslinked structures in asphalt in a reactor, which inhibited graphitization and achieved an increase in reversible capacity from 120.3 to 306.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Xu's work<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0020_R_d14617610e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Carbon">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> displayed a significant improvement in the disordered phases of HCs obtained from asphalt APO in mixture gas (O<sub>2</sub>/Ar = 1/4), showing an increase in the reversible capacity increase from 129.09 to 300.83&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Actually, the APO in air is very safe and the parameters (temperature, time, etc.) are easy to control, providing a guarantee for large-scale HCs production.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0021_R_d14617610e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0022_R_d14617610e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - New Carbon Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Hu's work,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0023_R_d14617610e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> by regulating the APO temperature in air, the HCs exhibited initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 88.6% with a reversible capacity of 300.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the oxygen-containing groups (OCGs) were mainly composed of C═O, C─O, and COOH. The introduction of oxygen significantly increased the capacity of asphalt-based HCs because OCGs pyrolysis created abundant micropores and expanded the interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0022_R_d14617610e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - New Carbon Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0024_R_d14617610e460" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Improving the oxygen-containing cross-linked structures is a feasible strategy to increase the capacity of asphalt-based HCs. Furthermore, the diffusion of oxygen molecules inside the asphalt preferentially crosslinks and polymerizes with the unsaturated structural units to form a cross-linked structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0025_R_d14617610e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Sci. Adv.">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0026_R_d14617610e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Energy Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the rich cross-linked structure in asphalt is likely to be associated with the oxygen content and the proportion of OCGs, and it is necessary to do further research.</p>
         
         <p>Based on previous reports, there is a universal knowledge that the layer spacing of HCs decreases as the pyrolysis temperature increases,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0027_R_d14617610e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. A">27</a>-<a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0031_R_d14617610e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the specific surface area also decreases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0032_R_d14617610e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0033_R_d14617610e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The smaller layer spacing has a greater potential energy, increasing the barrier for sodium ions to enter the carbon layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0034_R_d14617610e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Lett.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0035_R_d14617610e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Small">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fortunately, the low specific surface area reduces interfacial side reactions and improves the ICE of HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0036_R_d14617610e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0037_R_d14617610e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Mater. Chem. A">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The paradox is that the introduction of OCGs in the asphalt lead increases the layer spacing and specific surface area of the HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0019_R_d14617610e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0021_R_d14617610e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0022_R_d14617610e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - New Carbon Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This means that increasing ICE may be accompanied by a decrease in the sodium storage capacity of asphalt-based HCs. Due to differences in the thermal stability of different types of OCG (C═O, C─O, and C(O)─O),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0038_R_d14617610e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Pet. Chem.">38</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0039_R_d14617610e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Fuel">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the different decompositions take place at pyrolysis temperature, which inevitably affects the evolution of disordered phases and pores in asphalt-based HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0018_R_d14617610e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0020_R_d14617610e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Carbon">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Note that the electrolyte can freely travel through mesopores on HCs surfaces but not into micropores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0040_R_d14617610e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Energy Lett.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the abundant micropores in HCs are the key to increasing sodium storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0032_R_d14617610e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0041_R_d14617610e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the formation of abundant micropores induced during the pyrolysis process not only compensates for the loss of capacity caused by the narrowing of the carbon layer spacing but is also expected to achieve an increase in ICE.</p>
         
         <p>The random arrangement of carbon layers creates the complex structure of HCs, leading to many controversies in the exploration of sodium storage mechanisms. In 2000, Stevens et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0042_R_d14617610e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">42</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0043_R_d14617610e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> were the first to reveal the “insertion-filling” mechanism, explaining the behavior of sodium ions in glucose-based HCs. Differently, researchers<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0032_R_d14617610e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0044_R_d14617610e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Energy Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0045_R_d14617610e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Energy Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> proposed an “adsorption-filling” mechanism, suggesting that capacity above 0.1&nbsp;V was associated with defect adsorption, while capacity below 0.1&nbsp;V corresponded to nanopore filling. On the contrary, other works found “adsorption-insertion” mechanism for sodium storage in HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0045_R_d14617610e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Energy Mater.">45</a>-<a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0047_R_d14617610e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At present, some researchers found the “adsorption-insertion-filling” mechanism for sodium in HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0041_R_d14617610e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0048_R_d14617610e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Energy Storage Mater.">48</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0049_R_d14617610e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nano Lett.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even, it has been proposed that the sodium storage mechanism of HCs has sodium cluster formation in addition to the “adsorption-insertion-filling”.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0050_R_d14617610e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the effect of OCGs on the structure of asphalt-based HCs is unclear, and with the ambiguity of the storage mechanism of sodium ions, further investigation is necessary.</p>
         
         <p>In this present work, the asphalt-based HCs with excellent electrochemical performance are prepared by APO at specific temperatures in the air to inhibit graphitization during carbonization. Specifically, the OCGs are connected with the unsaturated aliphatic hydrocarbon side chains in the asphalt to form a disordered carbon skeleton. The high proportion of C(O)─O inhibits the growth of the carbon layer during pyrolysis, which promotes the formation of disordered phases and abundant micropores. The simultaneous increase in ICE, capacity, and transport kinetics of sodium ions in asphalt-based HCs is achieved by adjusting the carbon layer and micropore. The optimized HCs display excellent ICE of 86.14% with a high reversible capacity of 313.83&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and good cycle stability with 90.26% retention after 200 cycles, as well as high-rate capability with 140&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C. Furthermore, it also reveals the “adsorption-insertion-pores-filling” sodium storage mechanism. This study will play a positive role in reducing the cost of SIBs and developing strategies to improve the electrochemical performance of HCs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403084-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403084-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>To rapidly identify suitable APO temperature, the thermogravimetry/differential scanning calorimetry (TG-DSC) of asphalt was first carried out in the air from room temperature to 500&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information). The asphalt weight decreases slightly with the temperature rise to ≈100&nbsp;°C, attributing to the volatility of water or small organic molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0023_R_d14617610e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, ≈5% of weight gain the highest point at ≈360&nbsp;°C can be attributed to oxygen availability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0023_R_d14617610e576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After that an exothermic peak and rapid weight decrease are observed in the TG-DSC curves, suggesting that thermal decomposition dominates in asphalts at higher APO temperature (&gt;360&nbsp;°C).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0051_R_d14617610e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Fuel">51</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0052_R_d14617610e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Fuel">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, the APO temperature at 360&nbsp;°C is more appropriate. The elemental analyses show that the maximum oxygen content of asphalt is ≈28.64% at APO 15&nbsp;h (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting the best APO status with rich OGGs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0021_R_d14617610e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which will effectively inhibit graphitization during the carbonization process. Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) was used to identify the types of OCGs in asphalt. The FTIR spectra of AOP-0&nbsp;h show that the vibration bands at 740–870, 1170, 1440, and 1600&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to phenyl hydrogen (Ph─H), C─O, ─CH<sub>3</sub>, and aromatic C═C stretching (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0001">1a</a>), whereas the bands at 2920 and 3040&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are involved in aliphatic ─CH<sub>2</sub> symmetric stretching and ─CH<sub>3</sub> asymmetric stretching,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0012_R_d14617610e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">12</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0052_R_d14617610e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Fuel">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. After the APO treatment, the new vibration bands appear at ≈1700 and 1840&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to C(O)─O and C═O stretching, respectively, as well as the peak intensity of C─O bonds at 1170&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> increases. Furthermore, the peaks at 740–870, 1440, 2920, and 3040&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> weaken or even disappear, demonstrating that the oxygen molecules are preferentially combined with unsaturated functional groups (such as hydrocarbon groups) to form OCGs (C(O)─O, C═O, and C─O).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403084-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4c14a78c-d0d5-4d1d-9491-7be119ebbb3c/aenm202403084-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4c14a78c-d0d5-4d1d-9491-7be119ebbb3c/aenm202403084-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/547b621a-7cbd-4d7b-9764-b00f326f55bf/aenm202403084-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4c14a78c-d0d5-4d1d-9491-7be119ebbb3c/aenm202403084-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403084-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403084" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The evolution of OCGs in asphalt with the APO time in air analyzed from FTIR; b) High-resolution XPS C1s spectra of APO-t (<i>t</i> = 0–25&nbsp;h); c) Fitted area of OCGs based on high-resolution C1s spectra.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Subsequently, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) tests can further understand the changes in the type and content of OCGs in asphalt with APO time. The peaks ≈248.8 and 532.2&nbsp;eV in full XPS spectra correspond to O1s and C1s, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information). The fitting C 1s and O1s fine spectra in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0001">1b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1d</a> (Supporting Information) show that the OCGs in APO-0&nbsp;h consist mainly of C═O (287.9 and 531.5&nbsp;eV), C─O (286.2 and 532.6&nbsp;eV), C(O)─O (289.2 and 533.7&nbsp;eV), and H<sub>2</sub>O (535.1&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0053_R_d14617610e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Sci. Rep.">53</a>-<a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0055_R_d14617610e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Carbon">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After APO, the intensity of the C═O, C─O, and C(O)─O peaks increase significantly, indicating that the OCGs in asphalt consist mainly of C═O, C─O, and C(O)─O (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0001">1b</a>). Note that the C(O)─O has a 3D structure to form mutually intersecting 3D structures in asphalt.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0020_R_d14617610e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Carbon">20</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0056_R_d14617610e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Energy Fuels">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0001">1c</a>, the APO-15&nbsp;h sample displays the higher C(O)─O), meaning the formation of a highly disordered carbon skeleton in asphalt, which can effectively obstruct rearrangement and slippage of carbon layers during carbonization.</p>
         
         <p>The effect of OCG evolution on the asphalt-based HC structure was analyzed by X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman spectroscopy. The XRD pattern only appears with two peaks at ≈23° and 43°, matching with (002) and (100) crystal planes (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0002">2a</a>), respectively. Unlike the sharp peak of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-0&nbsp;h, the (002) peaks of other samples are broader and shift to a low angle, indicating that OCGs effectively prevent the graphitization of asphalt. Note that the asymmetry of broad (002) peaks suggests the coexistence of graphite-like phases and disordered phases in HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0048_R_d14617610e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Energy Storage Mater.">48</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0057_R_d14617610e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Chem. Eng. J.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To better investigate the variation of disordered phases, the (002) peaks are fitted and divided into disordered phases with a <i>d</i>-spacing larger than 0.36&nbsp;nm and graphite-like phases with a <i>d</i>-spacing lower than 0.36&nbsp;nm<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0034_R_d14617610e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Lett.">34</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0047_R_d14617610e696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0002">2a</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the average length along the <i>a</i>-axis (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>) and thickness along the <i>c</i>-axis (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>) of crystallite sizes in HCs were calculated via the Scherrer equation, as listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Among them, HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h possesses the smaller values of <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> and a higher proportion (63.89%) of disordered phases. This is attributed to the higher share of OCGs (C(O)─O), which inhibits the growth of carbon crystallite during carbonization, improving the probability of random arrangement of carbon layers. Apart from that, HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h has a larger average <i>d</i><sub>(002)</sub> of ≈3.80&nbsp;nm (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), which lays the foundations for increasing the sodium storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0034_R_d14617610e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the Lorentz function,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0058_R_d14617610e740" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the Raman spectra of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0−25&nbsp;h) can be fitted and deconvolved to four subpeaks, namely, <i>D</i>, <i>D</i><sub>2</sub>, <i>D</i><sub>3</sub>, and <i>G</i> band (1345, 1200, 1500, and 1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0002">2c</a>. Ordinarily, the <i>D</i> peak and <i>G</i> peak are associated with disordered structure and crystalline graphite, respectively, and the area ratio of the fitted <i>D</i> and <i>G</i> peaks (<i>A</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub>) characterizes the disorder degree of HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0048_R_d14617610e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Energy Storage Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h shows a maximum <i>A</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>A</i><sub>G</sub> value of ≈1.71, indicating a higher proportion of disordered phases in HCs, which agrees well with the XRD results.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403084-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7cee6979-36fc-4bf7-965f-20492432990c/aenm202403084-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7cee6979-36fc-4bf7-965f-20492432990c/aenm202403084-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c2b262d4-f3d3-4677-afa2-7d0462fedc37/aenm202403084-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7cee6979-36fc-4bf7-965f-20492432990c/aenm202403084-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403084-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403084" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns and c) Raman spectra and fitted curves of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0–25&nbsp;h); b) XRD patterns and d) Raman spectra and fitted curves of HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200–1600&nbsp;°C).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Based on the optimal APO time of 15&nbsp;h, the effect of carbonization temperature on asphalt-based HCs structure was also investigated. The carbonation processes and products of APO-0&nbsp;h and APO-15&nbsp;h were monitored synchronously by TG-DSC-FTIR, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). In the APO-0&nbsp;h pyrolysis process, the FTIR spectra display the vibration bands of ─CH<sub>2</sub> and ─CH<sub>3</sub> ≈2920 and 3040&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in the temperature range of 300–600&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b,c</a>, Supporting Information),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0051_R_d14617610e832" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Fuel">51</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0052_R_d14617610e832" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Fuel">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> corroborating the fracture of alkyl side chains in aliphatic hydrocarbons or the secondary pyrolysis of low molecular alkanes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0012_R_d14617610e837" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">12</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0052_R_d14617610e837" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Fuel">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It accounts for this exothermic peak in the DCS curve and the rapid decrease of the TG curve of APO-0&nbsp;h between 300 and 600&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information). As the temperature increases to 1500&nbsp;°C, the vibration bands appear at 3750, 1500, 670, and 2350&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in FTIR spectra corresponding to H<sub>2</sub>O, CH<sub>4</sub>, and CO<sub>2</sub>, and the heat absorption peaks appear ≈980, 1220, 1350, and 1470&nbsp;°C in DSC curve, indicating the melting and crystallization of the asphalt due to the fracture of OCGs (C═O, ─O─C).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0041_R_d14617610e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0059_R_d14617610e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J. Hazardous Mater.">59</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0060_R_d14617610e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Fuel">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Differently, the TG curve of APO-15&nbsp;h drops rapidly and falls slightly in the temperature range of 400–800 and 900–1500&nbsp;°C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a>, Supporting Information), which is mainly related to the pyrolysis OCGs ((C(O)─O) and (C═O, O─C), respectively<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0041_R_d14617610e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0059_R_d14617610e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J. Hazardous Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2d,e</a>, Supporting Information). What's more, the DCS curve of APO-15&nbsp;h is smoother, meaning that even if OCGs (C═O, C─O, and C(O)─O) break, the disordered carbon skeleton is largely maintained in asphalt. Notably, HCs with a larger <i>d</i>-spacing and pores are so easily obtained at low-temperature carbonization (≤1000&nbsp;°C), but exhibit low ICE and without plateau capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0047_R_d14617610e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0061_R_d14617610e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - ACS Appl. Nano Mater.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, to better understand the structure evolution of the HCs with carbonization temperature, HCs-T-15&nbsp;h were prepared at 1200, 1300, 1400, 1500, and 1600&nbsp;°C for material characterization.</p>
         
         <p>As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0002">2b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the (002) peaks of HCs gradually shift to higher diffraction angles as the temperature from 1200 to 1600&nbsp;°C, suggesting a decrease in average <i>d</i>-spacing of the carbon layer, which is unfavorable to improve the sodium storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0031_R_d14617610e889" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> values first decrease and then increase with temperature, and reach the minimum at 1400&nbsp;°C. This is associated with the decomposition of OCGs (C(O)─O) resulting in the collapse of the carbon skeleton to form smaller carbon layer units<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0048_R_d14617610e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Energy Storage Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the ordered connection of neighboring carbon layers because of OCGs (C─O, C═O) fracture,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0021_R_d14617610e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0062_R_d14617610e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nat. Commun.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). As smaller carbon layers are more favorable for random stacking, HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h display a higher proportion of amorphous phases (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0002">2b</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The morphology characteristics of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0–25&nbsp;h) and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200–1600&nbsp;°C) were observed by scanning electron microscope (SEM) in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. Compared to HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-0&nbsp;h with carbon sheet stacked blocks, other samples display irregular block particles, which is attributed to the fact that OCGs (C═O, C─O, and C(O)─O) inhibit the melting and recrystallization of asphalt. The differences in the microcrystalline structures of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0–25&nbsp;h) and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200–1600&nbsp;°C) were further discriminated by high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM), as displayed in <b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0003">3a–f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). After APO, OCGs (C═O, C─O, and C(O)─O)) induce the appearance of disordered phases (yellow square marker), and the locally ordered carbon layers are classified as graphite-like phases (green oval marker), as well as the carbon layers are stacked like a “house of cards” to form micropores (red triangle marker). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0003">3e</a>, HCs-1400-15&nbsp;h get a larger average <i>d</i>-spacing of ≈0.380&nbsp;nm and abundant micropores and disordered phases, which provide rich sodium storage sites in asphalt-based HCs. Note that the carbonation temperature also leads to the evolution of microcrystalline structures, as displayed in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0003">3d–f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c,d</a> (Supporting Information). With the carbonization temperatures from 1200 to 1600&nbsp;°C, the size of the carbon layer first increases and then decreases, and the average <i>d</i>-spacing gradually decreases from 0.384 to 0.357&nbsp;nm, consistent with the results of XRD (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Although HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h does not show the maximum interlayer spacing, the rich disordered phases, and micropores may compensate for the loss of interlayer spacing capacity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403084-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d4d1be60-3dae-4c69-b826-26722cccb588/aenm202403084-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d4d1be60-3dae-4c69-b826-26722cccb588/aenm202403084-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4ebc4406-34a7-4afd-9c69-9e350ef03420/aenm202403084-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d4d1be60-3dae-4c69-b826-26722cccb588/aenm202403084-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403084-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403084" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–f) HRTEM images of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (0, 5, and 25&nbsp;h) and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200, 1400, and 1600&nbsp;°C). Micropore characterization of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0, 5, and 25&nbsp;h) and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200, 1400, and 1600&nbsp;°C): g,h) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms (77&nbsp;K); i,j) CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms (273&nbsp;K); k,l) SAXS spectra.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption thermostatic lines of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0–25&nbsp;h) and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200–1600&nbsp;°C) exhibit a mixture of type I and type II in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0003">3g,h</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a,b</a> (Supporting Information), respectively, indicating the presence of both micropores and mesopores in the asphalt-based HCs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0045_R_d14617610e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Energy Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the specific surface area (<i>S</i><sub>BET</sub>), total pore volume (<i>V</i><sub>pore</sub>), and average pore size (<i>D</i><sub>pore</sub>) parameters calculated by the Brunauer-Emmet-Teller (BET) model are listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> first decreases and then increases with the APO time from 0 to 15&nbsp;h, and HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h has a smaller <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> of 4.997&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, attributing that OCGs refines the carbon chain (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) to surround by each other to form smaller spaces like a “house of cards” (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). From Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0003">3h</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), <i>V</i><sub>pore,</sub> and <i>D</i><sub>pore</sub> first decrease and then increase with the carbonization temperature, and reach a smaller at 1400&nbsp;°C, which is related to the collapse of the carbon skeleton and the growth of the carbon layer, respectively. Since the electrolyte can through mesopores larger than 2&nbsp;nm, excessive mesopores increase side reactions on the HC surface to consume sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0036_R_d14617610e1059" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0063_R_d14617610e1059" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Power Sources">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h with a small S<sub>BET</sub> of 4.997&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> will show high ICE.</p>
         
         <p>The CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption thermostatic experiments are tested to further analyze the micropores (&lt;1&nbsp;nm) of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0–25&nbsp;h) and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200–1600&nbsp;°C), as shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0003">3i,j</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7c,d</a> (Supporting Information), respectively, and the microporous parameters are calculated based on the density functional theory (DFT) model and listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). Surprisingly, the changes of <i>S</i><sub>DFT</sub> and <i>V</i><sub>pore</sub> show an opposite trend to <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> and <i>V</i><sub>pore</sub> with increasing the APO time and carbonization temperature. HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h shows the largest value of <i>S</i><sub>DFT</sub> and <i>V</i><sub>pore</sub>, indicating that the rich disordered phases and small carbon microcrystals promote the transformation of mesopores into micropores, which not only reduces the contact surface with electrolyte but also provides more sodium storage sites and more sodium diffusion channels.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0041_R_d14617610e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0062_R_d14617610e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nat. Commun.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The internal pores (closed pores) of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0–25&nbsp;h) and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200–1600&nbsp;°C) are analyzed by the small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS), as shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0003">3k,l</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7e–h, S8, S9</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The SAXS plot of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-0&nbsp;h is a tilted straight line, indicating a more perfect graphite structure. Conversely, a broad peak appears ≈0.1&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup> in the SAXS plot of the additional samples, which is typical for the presence of closed pores in HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0032_R_d14617610e1134" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to facilitate the analysis of the pore structure, it is generally considered that the micropores are approximately spherical and the pore sizes follow the Schulz–Zimm function,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0032_R_d14617610e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0041_R_d14617610e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7g,h</a> (Supporting Information), which illustrates the pore size distribution of the sample. From Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h owns the smaller <i>D</i><sub>closed pores</sub> of ≈0.618&nbsp;nm. Additionally, to further obtain the total closed pore volume (<i>V</i><sub>closed pore</sub>), the true density of HCs was tested using He based on Archimedes principle. Ideal graphite is considered to be without closed pores, and it has a true density of 2.26&nbsp;g&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup>. Thus, <i>V</i><sub>closed pore</sub> of HCs can be obtained by <i>V</i><sub>closed pore</sub> = 1/𝜌<sub>true</sub>−1/2.26,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0041_R_d14617610e1168" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as displayed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h has a larger <i>V</i><sub>closed pore</sub> ≈0.662&nbsp;g&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup>, confirming that abundant OCGs can promote the formation of abundant micropores, which provides a guarantee for increasing sodium storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0032_R_d14617610e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Small">32</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0062_R_d14617610e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nat. Commun.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the sodium storage capacity of the asphalt-based HCs, the electrochemical performances of the half-cells were initially tested. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h measured at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> are exhibited in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. The two broad reduction peaks are present at ≈0.2–0.6 and 0.7–1.0&nbsp;V (Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) in the first cycle of the negative sweep and disappear in the second cycle, except for HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-0&nbsp;h, relating to the formation of an irreversible solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on the HCs surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0064_R_d14617610e1199" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-0&nbsp;h (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information), the reduction peak at 0.2–0.6&nbsp;V has just weakened but does not disappear in the third cycle, attributing that the SEI layer continues to grow due to larger <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub> of HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0037_R_d14617610e1211" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Mater. Chem. A">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h plays a weaker reduction peak at 0.2–0.6&nbsp;V and a pair of the most elongated reversible redox peaks at 0.001–0.1&nbsp;V (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c</a>, Supporting Information), meaning weaker interfacial side reactions between HCs and electrolyte and the better insertion and extraction of the sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0023_R_d14617610e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0047_R_d14617610e1223" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>From the initial galvanostatic charge–discharge curves (<b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0004">4a</a>,<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0004">b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a,b</a>, Supporting Information), HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-0&nbsp;h delivers a low reversible capacity of 120.82&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and ICE of 49.75% (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Benefiting from suitable <i>d</i>-spacing, abundant micropores and lower <i>S</i><sub>BET</sub>, HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h exhibits a high ICE of 86.14% with a reversible capacity of 313.83&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (1 C = 0.279&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). A comparison of the electrochemical performances of asphalt-based HCs with other reported HCs in similar electrolytes is listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). Besides, the HRTEM images reveal that the high ICE of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h is related to the thin SEI of ≈10&nbsp;nm owing to low S<sub>BET</sub> (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The galvanostatic charge–discharge curves can be divided into two regions: a slope region in the range of 0.1–2.5&nbsp;V and a plateau region of low potential in the range of 0.001–0.1&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0041_R_d14617610e1269" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0057_R_d14617610e1269" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Chem. Eng. J.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0004">4c,d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c,d</a> (Supporting Information), HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-0&nbsp;h has almost no plateau capacity. Conversely, HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h has the highest plateau capacity of 216.77&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> which is 67.01% of the total capacity, attributing rich disordered phases and micropore storage of sodium. Furthermore, HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h reveals excellent long cyclic stability of capacity retention of 90.26% with a capacity of 292.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.1 C, which benefits from the formation of a thin and stable SEI layer after 2nd cycles. Apart from that, the thin SEI reduces the blockage of sodium ion insertion and extraction of the HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0031_R_d14617610e1285" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0004">4g,h</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12g,h</a> (Supporting Information), HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h has the highest sodium storage capacity at different current densities, especially at 5 C, the reversible capacity is ≈140&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This also benefits from the thinner carbon layer and smaller micropores shortening sodium ion to outlet distance and more sodium diffusion channels from abundant micropores on the surface of asphalt-based HCs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0041_R_d14617610e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0062_R_d14617610e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nat. Commun.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403084-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/589faed7-ec4b-4566-b865-d0f08909c87e/aenm202403084-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/589faed7-ec4b-4566-b865-d0f08909c87e/aenm202403084-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2d48cd26-891c-4f6f-a97e-d523aedc19ee/aenm202403084-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/589faed7-ec4b-4566-b865-d0f08909c87e/aenm202403084-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403084-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403084" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0, 5, and 25&nbsp;h) and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200, 1400, and 1600&nbsp;°C). a,b) The initial galvanostatic charge–discharge curves at 0.0279&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; c,d) Capacity contribution of the slop region and plateau region in the second cycle discharge curve; e,f) Cycling performance at 0.0279&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; g,h) Rate capability. Electrochemical performance of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15//O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> full cells: i) Cycling performance at 0.0240&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>; j) Rate capability.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To evaluate the practical application of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h, the full cell was assembled with O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as the cathode. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a> (Supporting Information), the full cell shows a higher ICE of 83.70% with a reversible capacity of 255.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass load of the anode), as well as a higher reversible capacity of ≈106.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is presented after 100 cycles at 0.0240&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0004">4i</a>). Figures&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0004">4j</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15c</a> (Supporting Information) show the sodium storage capacity at different current densities, and the full cell still has a reversible capacity of 130&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.120&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Besides, the electrochemical performance of the commercial HCs (Kuraray)// O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> full cell has been presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). The above results show that the asphalt-based HCs prepared in this work have broad application prospects.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above structural and electrochemical characteristics of asphalt-based HCs, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was carried out to understand the insertion and extraction behaviors of sodium ions. The diffusion coefficients <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> can be calculated based on Fick's second law with the simplified equation<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0048_R_d14617610e1395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Energy Storage Mater.">48</a>, <a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0065_R_d14617610e1395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Energy Mater.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), and the results of the GITT are shown in <b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0005">5a,b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information). Except for HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-0&nbsp;h, the GITT curves of other HCs show a similar change trend, indicating similar storage sodium ion behaviors. Above 0.1&nbsp;V, <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> decreases ≈0.80 and 0.55&nbsp;V, which is tied to the SEI formation preventing the diffusion of sodium ions on asphalt-based HCs surface. After that, <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> decreases sharply at 0.1–0.05&nbsp;V, characterizing the transport behavior of sodium ions between carbon layers needing to overcome energy barriers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0034_R_d14617610e1420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nano Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The rapid increase in <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> at the 0.05–0.001&nbsp;V stage suggests that the sodium ions overcome the barriers to enter the micropores. The GITT results tentatively confirm that the capacity of the asphalt-based HCs consists of surface adsorption, an insertion carbon layer, and a hole-filling storage sodium. Note that HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h presents higher <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>, which benefits from the weaker interfacial side reactions reducing the obstruction of sodium ions, the thin carbon layer, and abundant micropores reducing the transport distances of sodium ions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403084-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e1cc1b34-2afd-4a58-8d7d-7e07758dfb33/aenm202403084-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e1cc1b34-2afd-4a58-8d7d-7e07758dfb33/aenm202403084-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a690f070-7c06-480e-a0a2-54daac66014d/aenm202403084-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e1cc1b34-2afd-4a58-8d7d-7e07758dfb33/aenm202403084-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403084-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403084" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) Calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients during the first discharge and charge of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-t (<i>t</i> = 0–25&nbsp;h) and HCs-T-15&nbsp;h (<i>T</i> = 1200–1600&nbsp;°C); c) In-situ XRD contour pattern; d) In-situ XRD patterns of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h corresponding to different potentials during the first discharge-charge; e) Schematic diagram of the sodium storage stages of asphalt based-HCs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The in-situ XRD of HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h was tested in the first discharge-charge process to further investigate the sodium storage mechanism, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0005">5c,d</a>, and the XRD pattern of the blank Be window have been listed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). It can be observed that the (002) peaks at ≈23° change periodically during the discharge-charge process, indicating that sodium ion transport is a repeatable process. Above 0.1&nbsp;V, the position of the (002) peaks does not change, which indicates that sodium ions are adsorbed on the HCs surface, as shown in the light blue region of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0005">5e</a>. From 0.1 to 0.05&nbsp;V, the intensity of the (002) peak increases, and the position shifts to a lower angle, revealing the expansion of the <i>d</i>-spacing caused by the insertion of sodium ions in the carbon layers, as seen in the buff region of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0005">5e</a>. It is worth noting that when the potential continues to drop to 0.001&nbsp;V, the (002) peak maintains its status, and there is no Na metal peak,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403084-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403084-bib-0050_R_d14617610e1476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Energy Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which implies that the sodium ions perform a pore-filling behavior (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403084-fig-0005">5e</a> in light red region) rather than stacking to form superlattice sodium clusters. Overall, the mechanism of sodium storage in asphalt-based HCs is adsorption-insertion-pore filling, which is supported by the GITT results.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403084-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403084-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, asphalt-based HCs with excellent electrochemical properties were prepared by adjusting the APO time in the air at a specific temperature, as well as the carbonization. The oxygen-containing groups were introduced into the asphalt to form a disordered carbon skeleton for hindering graphitization. The pyrolysis of the abundant C(O)─O inhibits the growth of carbon layers, which promotes the formation of disordered phases and micropores in asphalt-based HCs. Besides, the optimal carbonization temperature of 1400&nbsp;°C facilitates the conversion of mesopores to micropores, which reduces the specific surface area favoring the formation of a thin and stable SEI layer. Moreover, the thinner carbon layers and abundant micropores provide more sodium diffusion channels to improve the kinetic behavior of sodium ion transport in asphalt-based HCs. Benefiting from structural optimization, HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h displays an excellent ICE of 86.14% with a high reversible capacity of 313.83&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and rate performance with 140&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;C. After 200 cycles, the capacity reaches 292.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with 90.26% capacity retention at 0.1 C. The HCs-1400&nbsp;°C-15&nbsp;h//O3-NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> full cell delivers a high ICE of 83.7% with a reversible capacity of 255.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a long cyclic stability. Based on the correlation between the microstructure and sodium storage behaviors of asphalt-based HCs, the “adsorption-insertion-pores-filling” sodium storage mechanism is proposed. This work will offer new ideas for improving the sodium storage capacity of HCs obtained from other precursors, like coal and resin.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
